Based on the 1982 children's novel of the same name, which also inspired a stage production, War Horse follows the travails of a spirited horse named Joey during World War I as he is raised by a farmboy, sold off to a soldier, and marched into the middle of battle. Joey endures various hardships but endures through it all, inspiring those around him to greater things. Characterized by polished filmmaking, old-fashioned storytelling, and a certain trademark sentimentality, War Horse won many fans when it opened during the holiday season and six Academy Award nominations, including Best Picture. Each of the 6 DVDs are contained within a book like container.  The discs themselves need to be slid out of the 'page' containing the disc but this was not difficult to do and it was easy to avoid placing your fingers on the face of the disc itself. Since the first theatrical release, there have been a great many other films that have copied the infamous 'monster punching out through the chest' effect.  In fact, that has become so very ho hum as it  has been copied just too many times by directors who could not rely upon their own creativity and had to copy others.<br />Both the first Alien and 2nd Aliens films retain their quality with excellent video transfers showing great details in the dark and shadow areas.  The ability to show detail in shadows, often dependent upon the T.V.'s ability to pass what is known as 'Pluge' will make a huge difference in how you view any of these films.  Some viewers might complain that they could not see much detail in the dark scenes but that will not be the fault of the Blu ray transfer, but the fault of their set's inability to pass pluge.  Both of these first two movies have a great deal of extra footage added in that will certainly enhance each film.  The 'deleted' and now added in footage simply expands the scenes they are in and are well worth having. The audio is equal to the first two, again, with good use of left and right front channels, sub and little use of the rear surrounds.  This is the only reason I gave the anthology on blu ray 4 stars instead of 5.  I appreciate when producers make accurate, albeit more expensive, use of the surrounds and though the audio is DTS HD, I believe they may have scrimped a bit in the sound mixing.<br /><br />The final film, Alien Resurrection, was a clever reboot of the Alien series and provides a fine take on film.  Interesting that in the directors commentary, he clearly states that the original theatrical release IS THE DIRECTOR'S cut but he added unseen footage to it anyway for this Blu ray release.  There is an excellent video transfer and the audio did make better use of the rear surrounds tho, again, somewhat sparingly.<br /><br />Seeing Sigourney Weaver age from the first through the 4th films was an unexpected delight. She literally grew into her role as the progression of films continued.  In Alien Resurrection she was still beautiful but almost frightening in her countenance. B006L3RZ26|5.0|Hearing Xuefei Yang's Bach Concertos, you could almost be convinced that Bach had written specifically for the guitar.  The truth is, Bach wrote for the ages and great artists such as Yang are the sands that keep the hourglass endlessly flowing.  She has a rare musical talent that becomes even more so when it comes to reinterpretation; she is adept at providing a fresh angle while not losing the original composition.  With the Elias Ensemble, she's come up with arrangements that sound very natural as guitar concertos; whether it is scalar runs in track 3 and 4 (Harpsichord Concerto No. 1 in D Minor) or the crisp hammer-ons and pull-offs in track 6 (Violin Concerto 1 Allegro), its more than top notch guitar playing, it becomes transcendent due to her openness to both musical and technical possibilities.  There are understated percussive sounds, or little twists on technique unlike anything I've heard in previous readings of these pieces regardless of the instrument.  It all seems innate to the music though and there is an effervescence that is intoxicating.  Yang is involved in a very open give and take with the compositions; she is taking chances, but the negotiation is respectful of the original pieces.  There is an air of discovery that these subtle inventions grant.  For a good example of this listen to Violin Concerto No. 2 in E major Allegro.  It is as full and strident as ever, but the sustained note (impossible on the classical guitar) about 45 seconds into the Adagio becomes a trill, or half step interval that highlights the vernacular of the guitar.  Other guitarists have substituted whole notes plucked once over each measure of sustain, so even among her peers on the guitar, Yang is providing an arresting alternative to either that or even a straight tremelo.  Think of how many violinists have stated their souls in this note alone and it becomes a defining moment.<br /> Her voicing and accentuation in Violin Sonata in G minor BWV1001 Fugue are very much representative of how she brings a piece to life.  Whether subtle or overt, there is always a forward motion and her artistic choices seem to be an expression of divine will at times.  Yang can routinely infuse influences from many different sources and though the Adagio from Violin Sonata in G minor BWV1001 may have subdued tinges of Spanish and Asian colors, this is the most straightforward I've heard her play.  Over all there is a naturalism that brings both solo and ensemble works to the ear unadorned.  This richly informed musicality along with her fluid technique and innovative arrangements translate into timeless beauty.presentative of how she brings a piece to life.  Whether subtle or overt, there is always a forward motion and her artistic choices seem to be an expression of divine will at times.  Yang can routinely infuse influences from many different sources and though the Adagio from Violin Sonata in G minor BWV1001 may have subdued tinges of Spanish and Asian colors, this is the most straightforward I've heard her play.  Over all there is a naturalism that brings both solo and ensemble works to the ear unadorned.  This richly informed musicality along with her fluid technique and innovative arrangements translate into timeless beauty.

B005XBA9Y8|5.0|I was a fan of the Mercury Living Presence series back in the 1960's, when the albums were on vinyl. This set of course has transfers from the original tapes (and in some cases the original 35mm magnetic films). The sound is brilliant, rivaling modern digital recordings. The original masters were made using a 3-microphone system that was elegant in its simplicity, and great attention was paid to the entire recording sound chain. Not a hint of overmodulation anywhere, only the slightest audible tape hiss, and yet the orchestral pieces have amazing dynamic range and preserve the brilliance of the high frequencies.<br /><br />The works are diverse, featuring many modern composers, stellar orchestras, pianists, guitarists, and even marching bands. At only about a couple of dollars per CD, this collection offers great value and is not to be missed.

0062073478|5.0|This is a classic Agatha Christie murder mystery/suspense novel.  Ten people are invited to Indian Island by an anonymous host.  They all come to the island for various reasons: employment, friendship, entertainment.  When they arrive, they realize they've all been summoned there to account for crimes of murder they've committed but were never sentenced for.  As they begin to die, one by one, they realize the deaths follow the pattern of the nursery rhyme, \\"Ten Little Indians.\\"  Fear turns to suspicion as the guests turn on each other, sure that the killer must be one of them. Who will remain when the last little Indian is alone?<br /><br />I've taught this book to my junior high PreAP students with excellent results. Some of the topics we have used this novel to explore include: synonyms, metaphors, idioms, theme, motif, symbolism, SAT vocabulary, and the mystery genre.  I found an online book trailer to engage my students in the beginning.  Once the murders begin, the action is fast-paced, and students engage in a guessing game trying to see who can figure out the killer's identity.  Fortunately, the ending cannot be predicted, and it comes as a great surprise.  I loved it, my students loved it, and I'm sure any lover of suspense will love it, too.

0307959856|3.0|I am a devoted reader of both Jane Austen and PD James, and looked forward to reading Death Comes to Pemberly. As it turned out,  I was so astounded by how badly James handled  this little excursion into the Regency world that  I repeatedly found myself wondering if this weren't something James wrote BEFORE becoming a successful author.  Is this, perhaps, some piece of Jamesian juvenalia that was brushed off to make a book (and a quick buck)?  I initially gave the book two stars, but concluded that it is at least a little more literate and intelligent than that.  But the book has none of the wit, warmth, charm, humor or insight into human nature of Austen, nor any semblance of James's usual deft plotting, suspenseful story-telling, or psychological character portraits.  It is, in fact, a mess, and a sad mess at that.  There is virtually no action, and no suspense whatsoever.  A man is killed in a wood early in the book, and pretty much nothing happens after that.  Lots and lots of exposition, endless repetition that should have been edited out, a great deal of hand-wringing, and a few frankly lame discursions into various Austen books (gratuitous references  to the families from \\"Persuasion\\"  and \\"Emma\\"  might have been fun in a wittier book, here they were simply filler that struck a jarring note), but no plot development, no story arch, no character growth, no twists, and no payoff at the end. Along the dreary way, James manages to make every single member of the original cast a great deal less likable than they were when Austen created them.  Jane calls Elizabeth Elizabeth (never Lizzy), they refer to each other's husbands as Mr Darcy and Mr Bingley (despite being brother and sister for eight years now), and both are utterly conventional, boring and insipid;  Darcy apparently has regressed and become insecure to boot; and Colonel FitzWilliam is made unrecognizable, and thoroughly uncongenial, for reasons known only to Ms. James.  I can only say I hope at least she thought those reasons  sufficient, as I can't see the justification for it.  With 20 pages to go I was still foolishly hoping for something to happen, for a plot twist that would demonstrate that it was PD James who had written this book, and make my attention to the seemingly pointless plot thus far worthwhile.  Nothing happened; and the the revelations of those last few pages weren't worth the candle.  The reviews that claim that this book is as good as anything PD James has ever written do James an outrageous injustice; she has written infinitely better things.  If you want a good mystery, read one of those.  If you wish Austen had written more, read Georgette Heyer or Stephanie Barron.  Contrary to the fulsome \\"professional\\" reviews, this book will satisfy neither mystery buffs nor Austenites.

0553807919|5.0|The Whole-Brain Child is about relationships. Foremost, it is about the relationship between our interactions with our children and their neurological development. More specifically, this book is about connections. Throughout the connections we share with our children, we have the opportunity to directly influence their brain's architecture. Likewise, as we consider the ways in which children's brains process our interactions, we have the opportunity to intentionally support an architectural framework that will last a lifetime. Simple ideas with profound implications, such as Name it to Tame it, Engage, don't Enrage, and Connect through Conflict, are but a few of the strategies the authors present in a thoughtful and accessible format, one that respects the role of relationships within families. One of the best aspects of this book is the manner in which Siegel and Bryson present the most current findings in neurological research: the integrity of the research is in place and the language of delivery allows us to actually understand and address the implications.<br />What I most appreciate about this book is the dedication to families' experiences with their children and the realization that while we all pledge to do our best each and every day, we all will have moments, days, stages where our best is exhausted, on hiatus, and for the moment we are only human. In this book, Dr. Siegel and Dr. Bryson remind us that we have an opportunity to work in the direction of children's growth and development and that we have every right to know and understand how children's brains organize and develop based on our interactions, reactions, and relationships with one another.<br />The Whole-Brain Child is the closest I will get to a manual about how to help my child develop internal resources to live and grow in a diverse world, to engage meaningfully in relationships, and to learn with an open-heart and open-mind.

B000MCIBE8|5.0|The Doors' debut rewrote the songbook for how rock bands could behave. Essentially a power trio without a bass player, these four men bent and twisted conventional rock forms and dropped a fully-formed masterpiece on the first try. Jim Morrison exuded sensuality and anti-authoritarianism from the moment he opens his mouth on \\"Break On Through To The Other Side\\" and commands his followers to give up convention and follow him into his lizard kingdom.<br /><br />Drummer John Densmore and guitarist Robby Krieger added texture more than structure, but the secret weapon was keyboardist Ray Manzarek. His organ and electric piano work often formed the foundations for the rest of the band to improvise on top of. The Doors could then run the gamut of stunning pop melodies (\\"Light My Fire\\") to the mystical (\\"Crystal Ship\\") all the way to the apocalyptic retelling of the oedipal complex, \\"The End.\\" The were also fearless enough to take a Kurt Weill song and utterly reclaim it, or make Willie Dixon's \\"Back Door Man\\" into a psychedelic blues rivaling Janis Joplin.<br /><br />If there was any problem with \\"The Doors,\\" it was that it's too perfect; The Doors had a hard time living up to the aftershock. Still, this was the birth of an American Classic Band, and \\"The Doors\\" maintains its potency 40 years later.

B00166BL6Y|4.0|For some reason Jethro Tull kind of fell off my radar after just a few albums.  But still I had a certain affection for them back in the day--coupled with a kind of gratitude, you could say.  In the late '60s/early 70's, when I was growing up in southern Maine, there weren't all that many major label acts that would play in Portland or its environs.  Nowadays it's not at all uncommon, and in fact, less than 10 years after I first saw Tull at the Portland City Hall auditorium (not at all a bad venue, by the way),  the city's Civic Center was built, and it no longer was the case that you pretty much had to travel to Boston to see a major act.<br /><br />My friends and I caught Jethro Tull in the summer of '69, just before the release of their second album (and before OUR last year in high school).  We'd been listening to the first record, THIS WAS JETHRO TULL, for months prior,  so naturally the concert promised to be one of the summer's highlights (if not the whole year's).  But there was always that concern back then, that a \\"big group\\" might slack off for an audience in the hinterlands.  Jimi Hendrix had played a less than 45 minute set the year before.  There was no need to worry about Tull though, they were still a hungry band playing for a hungry audience.  They gave it pretty much their all, as I remember it, and the audience ate it up.<br /><br />Jethro Tull was at that point, Ian Anderson's group,  and I personally was unaware that it had ever been anything but.  Since the band dutifully performed a great deal of the first album's material in their set that night,  I don't think I would have ever given a thought to the fact that the group I was seeing on stage that night was, in many ways, a substantially different group than the one behind THIS WAS.  I'm not even sure I'd have recognized the name Mick Abrahams, come to think of it.  As far as I knew, Jethro Tull was all about the eccentric guy with the flute, the one standing on one leg half the time.<br /><br />And, yeah, Ian Anderson did have his hand in shaping the sound of this album, but its rootsier side most likely had a lot to do with Abrahams' blues vision.  If reports of their not getting along are accurate, it can still be viewed as a real shame (some 40 years on) that their professional association was short lived. Their give and take is evident, and the album, as a whole, worked very well indeed. This is not just standard issue British late 60s blues-rock by any means.  Anderson's artistic restlessness seems to have guaranteed that.  On the other hand, the bluesy touches that Abrahams brought to the table are pretty tasty too.<br /><br />You could say that the mix of American styles (jazz/blues) and British folk roots were what made the band somewhat unique.  I mean, you could say Traffic was covering the same turf, and they WERE.  But both groups were so distinctive that, for a time, it seemed like jazz-blues-rock-with-a-hint-of-folk was likely to be the next big thing.  It WAS a thing, of course, if not THE big thing.<br /><br />The 40th anniversary special edition may be mainly a \\"fans only\\" affair, with its multiple renditions of the same basic songs spread over two disks.  Devotees will no doubt welcome having both the original mono tracks AND the stereo remixes in the same package--to say nothing of some quality BBC live takes and other bonus material.  Very casual listeners will get a deja-deja-vu sensation if they play the records straight through.  The fact that the last track on Disc 2 (\\"Christmas Song\\") is preceded by its stereo mix a mere four tracks prior can be a bit disconcerting. Like so many special editions (and box sets), this is one package that may benefit from being listened to in discrete segments.<br /><br />Personally, I'm only too glad to have  the chance to get re-acquainted with the early Tull and find myself asking why they  didn't actually rate a little higher in my personal hierarchy. Who can say? All I know is that now thatwe're ALLstarting to look a bit more like the band did on the original cover shot, I'm finding it a most welcome re-connection.we're ALLstarting to look a bit more like the band did on the original cover shot, I'm finding it a most welcome re-connection.

0439023491|3.0|I won't say \\"Catching Fire\\" was a bad novel. It wasn't. Though it lacked the brilliance of its predecessor, it was still a very compelling story with which I spent some enjoyable hours. Its problem is that it was something of a rehash.<br /><br />The first part of the book, with Katniss and Peeta back at home in District 12 and trying adapt to their new/old lives, navigate their conflicted relationship, and put the Hunger Games behind them while the upcoming Victory Tour looms over them, is excellent. The Victory Tour sequence itself was a great read. But then the author (and here are some brief spoilers) contrives a plot device that puts Katniss and Peeta back in the arena for the 75th Hunger Games.<br /><br />This was a disappointment to me because it wasn't the story I wanted to read. Far more interesting to me would have been to read about Katniss and Peeta dealing firsthand with the horrors of mentoring when the next Reaping took place. Think where the author could have gone with that: Prim could have been selected again, and Katniss wouldn't have been allowed to take her place a second time. Or one of Peeta's siblings, or one of Gale's (Gale being past the age for the reaping himself). Or perhaps Katniss's friend Madge who gave her the mockingjay pin. Things could have been complicated still further by having one of Gale's and one of Peeta's siblings both be tributes (I could see the Capitol engineering that). 0393072231|4.0|Michael Lewis can tell a story like no other.  In fact, even before I finished reading his \\"The Big Short,\\" I wanted to work the book into every conversation I had. The story was that interesting and compelling.  Anyone who can take the financial crisis of the last few years, find a story in it that centers around subprime mortgages and shorting the market (if you understand what that means and how to do it, you're more than a step ahead of me and about anyone else I've mentioned it to over the last couple weeks), and then make it interesting to the lay reader deserves to be read.<br /><br />In many ways (if not all ways), the stock market is a giant enigma to me, which suggests how Winston Churchill once described Russia: \\"I cannot forecast to you the action of Russia. It is a riddle, wrapped in a mystery, inside an enigma; but perhaps there is a key. That key is Russian national interest.\\" If there is a key to the enigma, with that riddle wrapped in a mystery inside it, that is the stock market, perhaps the key is found in the self-interest of the individuals participating in the market. Not just the stock and bond traders that made up the named characters of \\"The Big Short,\\" but even the home buyers and owners that took out second, third, and fourth mortgages, bought two, three, and four \\"investment\\" properties, the loan originators who sought them out and offered no interest loans, and the banks that sliced up the loans to fill tranches (there's another cryptic word for you) for trading as bonds to and between the financial houses on Wall Street.<br /><br />In other words, as Gordon Gecko might say: greed. Greed of bonds traders, floor traders, home buyers, loan originators, strawberry pickers, and house cleaners. Greed by just about everyone involved, from the top all the way down to the bottom.<br /><br />Lewis, known for his writing in \\"The Blind Side\\" and in \\"Money Ball,\\" with \\"The Big Short: Inside the Doomsday Machine\\" returns to his original stomping ground covered in \\"Liar's Poker: Rising Through the Wreckage on Wall Street.\\" Finding the few who saw the crash coming, he pulls together a narrative about those who anticipated the crash and saw it coming. While so many were getting rich off trading subprime mortgage based bonds, a few individuals realized that the underlying assets to the housing bubble were not stable and predicted that as interest rates on adjustable rate mortgages became due, defaults would sky-rocket and the bonds' values would crash.<br />And then they bet against it, cashing in lucratively when the predicted defaults began.<br /><br />What makes the story fascinating, of course, is the attention to the often colorful and more than slightly eccentric personalities that comprised the handful of individuals in the story. The stock market is difficult to understand for a simple reason-its workings are data driven and few pay the price to understand the numbers and analysis behind the market. In contrast, those who did, and those who got lucky, were often driven by a narrow-minded focus the data. From a neurologist turned hedge fund manager diagnosed with Aspergers in the midst of the story to a couple of young college grads who all but lucked into it, to a loud mouthed malcontent who made a habit of sticking it to the big wigs on Wall Street who lost investors money to the crisis, the \\"The Big Short\\" is replete with Lewis's deft story telling.<br /><br />Whether you are interested in finance or just looking for a great story, \\"The Big Short\\" is worth the time to read. I listened to it in the car, and often found myself sitting in the driveway waiting for the end of a section. More, it introduced me to concepts and interests that I'm exploring further in other books. Read in conjunction with \\"Too Big to Fail,\\" which I read last year, it provides an up close look at what was going on and why our economy is dragging through the longest recession in a generation.<br /><br />One last observation: one aspect that this book noted that continues to shock me no matter how many times I hear it over the last four years is how people with little or no credit history or ability managed to get so much financing. From immigrant strawberry pickers in California with $750,000 mortgages to house cleaners in Brooklyn with four and five town homes, obtuse financial incentives to originators and investors alike distorted the market in ways that were dangerous to all of us. While I look forward to other financial histories for other perspectives, Lewis seems to make clear that it wasn't so much the free market that failed, but the financial incentives that were built into it. In the end, the old adages \\"if it's too good to be true, it probably is\\" and \\"there's no such thing as a free lunch\\" seem even more relevant today than ever. Be careful when the snake oil salesman comes calling. He may not have your best interests at heart, and he may not even know it himself.ect that this book noted that continues to shock me no matter how many times I hear it over the last four years is how people with little or no credit history or ability managed to get so much financing. 0061564893|1.0|On a scale of 1 to 10, this book is junk.<br />_______________<br />It's rare that I would waste space blasting a book. Life is short and time is a scarce resource. I'd rather just drop a book unworthy of finishing and move on to a new one. This time, though, I think 1421 merits further explanation because of the sensational success it has experienced worldwide.<br /><br />Simply put, 1421 is junk history posing as \\"real history.\\" Gavin Menzies has spun a fantastical and interesting tale out of the very real events surrounding the massive Chinese treasure fleets of 1421. His thesis-that the Chinese discovered the New World in the 1420s, mapped it, and that it was their maps that European explorers used when sailing for the New World (including, he argues, Columbus).<br /><br />Built by a Ming emperor to gather in tribute from the ends of the Earth, the fleet was one of the last acts of imperial hubris. Shortly after it set sail, the emperor died. His son, in replacing his father's policies, had the fleets destroyed upon their return, along with records gathered during the voyage. Starting with that sparse introduction, Menzies proceeds to gather bits and pieces of evidence stretching from China itself to the Indian subcontinent, from the Congo to Patagonia and beyond, and levies the evidence to tell a tale of the massive Chinese fleet charting the New World the greater part of a century before Columbus set sail in 1492.<br /><br />It is an extremely interesting and, if it were true, a ground breaking discovery and thesis. Perhaps it is true. But likely, it is not.<br />As I started reading it, the first question that came to mind for me was this: in the almost six centuries since these events happened, why has no one else suggested that the Chinese arrived first? Menzies explanation is that historians generally lack the skill set necessary to uncover the truth, a skill set that he has as a former captain in the British Navy. Unlike most historians, Menzies argues, he can read a chart, understand what he's looking at, and glean from these 15th century charts things that no historian would otherwise notice.<br /><br />Yeah. It's a bit of a stretch. I would be surprised to find that no historian has ever had the skill set to learn maritime charts and understand how to read them (heck, Theodore Roosevelt when only an undergraduate student at Harvard, researched and wrote a book of naval strategy -- \\"The Naval War of 1812\\"-that became a classic and a text-book used by both the US and British navies for decades after it was published). That being said, I gave Menzies the benefit of the doubt. I've long been intrigued with China and its history, and I think I wanted to believe that history as we have been taught might not be true. How interesting would it be for America to have been discovered by the Chinese?  Nothing like a bit of sensationalism to sell books.<br /><br />As I read, though, red flags continued to pop up. Out of only sparse details, Menzies would assert \\"conclusive proof\\" that his theories were finding relevance. Finally, over two hundred pages in, I decided to check into what critical review might have said about his methods and evidence. I reasoned that if Menzies is correct, or even has a good theory, then the academic community would support his findings with further research. I went to the internet.<br /><br />Critical acclaim was anything but what I found. In addition to finding entire sites dedicated to debunking Menzies myths, I also found that historical lectures had been given explaining and demonstrating that what Menzies proposed was just that-a proposal. Be it even true, the evidence was not there, not was the reasoning clearly logical.<br /><br />For example:<br />-Menzies claims that Chinese anchors have been found off of the coast of California, but fails to document them.<br />-1421 says that Chinese DNA is found in North America natives, but fails to account for the influx of Chinese immigrants in the 17th century.<br />-Menzies finds what he claims are chickens unique to Asia living in Peru, but fails to note that Peru exported millions of tons of silver to China and brought back silk and porcelain (and presumably other things, like, for example, chickens) throughout the heyday of the Spanish during the 16th through 17th centuries.<br /><br />And that's just to start.<br /><br />Historian Kirstin A. Seaver says, in dissecting claims about the Chinese in Vinland:<br /><br />\\"The study of history is likely to reward anyone willing to undertake it in a quest for better understanding of who they are, how they became what they are, and what they might hope to become. The manufacture of a history that never existed rewards only those who make money by deceiving the public.\\"<br /><br />If 1421 is true, Menzies has not found the evidence to support it. If it is false, it's junk and a waste of time to read. Further, it perpetuates a falsehood that makes the acquisition knowledge about history-real, boring, dry and factual history-that much harder to grasp.e 17th century.<br />-Menzies finds what he claims are chickens unique to Asia living in Peru, but fails to note that Peru exported millions of tons of silver to China and brought back silk and porcelain (and presumably other things, like, for example, chickens) throughout the heyday of the Spanish during the 16th through 17th centuries.<br /><br />And that's just to start.<br /><br />Historian Kirstin A. At times during reading I really thought this lady was crazy, but I couldn't help but admire her strength. I'm not saying I want to be like her but I do respect her. Don't assume your child is weak. Assume strength. Children live up to the expectations parents set for them (I've seen examples this not only in this book but also in lives of friends and family, both Westerners and Asians).<br />2. Teach your children work ethics. For Chua, she chose piano and violin as ways for her children to learn work ethics, but those are not for everyone. Whatever the outlet is, don't let your child quit because it's hard. Nothing is fun until you are good at it, and you won't be good at it until you practice, and children don't practice on their own.<br />3. Verbal encouragement gives children confidence, but results give them more confidence. They see for themselves what they can do which in turn creates inner motivation.<br /><br />Growing up in two different cultures, my dad frequently said to me, \\"There are goods and bads in both cultures; take only the good.\\" And at the close of this book, Chua was coming to this understanding as well (albeit very hesitantly but Chua even realizes that and makes fun of herself for it). It was the love for her younger daughter and  the fear of losing her that Chua put down her iron will and questioned her Chinese way of parenting.  And like any good parent, whether they are Asians or Westerners, she learned that the most important thing in the world is the relationship she has with her own children.

B006PGL7OQ|3.0|There are NO SPOILERS here!<br /><br />I knew nothing about this movie when I ordered it on Netflix other than these two things:<br /><br />1) the trailer looked interesting, and<br /><br />2) Michael Shannon intrigues me<br /><br />On that basis, I ordered \\"Take Shelter,\\" and while I can not say I loved it, I can not say I hated it either.  Hence the three stars.<br /><br />Understand this: it's a good, thoughtful movie, but the unhurried, deliberate pace is only for those who like serious films more interested in building characterization than a frenetic plot.<br /><br />I do believe this movie is well made in the sense that it gives us a realistic construction of a man on the edge and does so within the confines of a middle income working class family in a midwestern state not prone toward hysteria or even colorful words and sustained monologues.<br /><br />This is, in fact, the exact opposite of a David Mamet film, wherein words are batted around like a Jai Alai ball.  There are long scenes in which Michael Shannon slumps on a chair looking glum, and others stare at him.  LONG scenes.<br /><br />But understand they are appropriate to the seriousness of the subject.  It has the feel of an indie film on within tight funding, and writer/director Jeff Nichols is able to do a good job within an apparently low budget creating apocalyptic visions that seem to exist solely within the head of character Curtis LaForche, played by Michael Shannon.  (Is the name \\"LaForche\\" important: a misspelling of \\"fork,\\" as in perhaps a character in the fork in the road, deciding where to go?)<br /><br />The fact that he has these vivid dreams not only when he sleeps, but also when his eyes are wide open and he is awake fully awake is complicated by the additional fact that his mother is in a care facility because when she was approximately his age she had a breakdown due to her own paranoid schizophrenia.<br /><br />Given the obvious misgivings he must himself obsess over, Curtis feels the less said, the better, except that his wife--well played by Jessica Chastain--has noticed his odd behavior and is seriously frightened for AND of him.  Trying her best to deal with a deaf daughter who needs an operation, and the fact that the only money they can scrape together through his job, odd work, and yard sales is being funneled away to build a shelter he believes necessary to shield his family from the upcoming disaster he foresees.<br /><br />Nichols is not interested in making a sci-fi film, but in constructing a careful moment-by-moment character study of a man on the edge, as upset as he is by his visions of the end of the world as he is that it all may simply be his descent into madness, just like his mother.  Chastain is also portrayed as a fully realized character who is trying so hard to do the right thing: to stand by her man as he crumbles before her very eyes.<br /><br />I had seen only one other film with Michael Shannon: the EXTREMELY odd \\"Bug,\\" which should indeed creep you out if you ever do see it.  He is quite haunting there, too, as he portrays a man who may or may not be there to rescue Ashley Judd.  It's all a testament to his face: a large misshapen head like Dudley Do-Right's after a catastrophic motorcycle accident.  His jutting jaw and obsessed eyes register misgivings like a seismograph, giving off a look that is equally paranoic and frightening.<br /><br />So see \\"Take Shelter\\" if you are interested in watching a well made movie about people, and only secondarily in watching a movie that may or may not be about a disaster.  It takes forever to get where it wants to go, but rest assured this movie delivers a punch when it does.<br /><br />If, however, you do not like long character studies, steer clear. This is shown in the play as well.) 0307352145|5.0|One-third to one-half of the people we interact with on a daily basis have introverted personalitiesquiet, reflective, sensitive, drawing energy from solitude. Yet, as author Susan Cain reveals in her new book QUIET: THE POWER OF INTROVERTS IN A WORLD THAT CAN'T STOP TALKING, it can be quite challenging to live as an introvert in a world that is characterized by what she calls the \\"Extrovert Ideal.\\" Whether in school, business, or religious institutions, people are often expected to be outgoing and life-of-the-party types, and this is viewed highly, while the quiet and reflective types are often viewed as socially flawed. Introverts often grow up in a culture where they are encouraged to become pseudo extroverts to be successful or just to get by.<br /><br />As an introvert, I know how easy it can be to view introversion as a weakness to be overcome. I can recall many times growing up when people would comment on how quiet I was as if it was a bad thing and the struggle to be quick on my feet in conversations with people. For several years I was a youth minister in a church culture where youth ministers were expected to be clear extroverts. Just a quick glance at youth minister job listings reveals the desire of most churches to have someone who is \\"outgoing.\\" While I've learned to do what needs to be done as an introvert, there have been many times when I've viewed my introversion as a crippling weakness. That is, until I think about my three-year-old daughter who is also an introvert. I listen to the same comments said about her that I've heard all my life, yet I know she's exactly as God created her to be, which means that introversion is vital to who she is. And vital to who I am.<br /><br />QUIET is a timely exploration of the advantages of being an introvert in a world that is often dominated by the Extrovert Ideal. It is a call for introverts to embrace who they are and call upon the strength of their personalities to be successful and make a marked impact upon the world. Through compelling research in the fields of psychology and neuroscience, as well as real-life examples of some of the most influential introverts, Susan Cain reveals that introversion is not a weakness, but instead a genuine personality type that has a distinctive set of strengths that are just as needed as those of the often more gregarious extroverted types.<br /><br />What are some of these advantages to being an introvert? Introverts enjoy being alone and drawing energy from solitude. They're often very reflective, careful, and resilient. This combination often means that introverts are some of the most creative people in the world. Introverts have given us Harry Potter, the iPhone, Google, the theory of gravity, and The Cat in the Hat. QUIET opens with the inspiring story of Rosa Parks who relied on her quiet strength to make a change in the racial discrimination of the 1950s.<br /><br />Cain guides introverts on how to rely on their free will to develop extroverted traits that are genuine to who they are and useful in certain contexts. This is called free trait theory. Though we'll never be genuinely naturally outgoing, we can find things that we're passionate about that help us to interact successfully within the Extrovert Ideal.<br /><br />Introverts are often erroneously viewed as anti-social. I love when Susan Cain points out that introverts aren't anti-social, but differently social. Cain reveals how introverts can be socially strong by relying on our inherent \\"soft power\\" and our ability to ask questions and listen attentively.<br /><br />One of the most helpful parts of the book that I appreciated the most was the chapter on raising introverted children. We know this means raising her with her introversion as an intentional focus.<br /><br />QUIET gives mehope for people like me and the many other introverts who struggle within an extroverted culture. 0439023521|5.0|In a dystopian future world, a plentiful society exists in a place called the Capitol which oversees 12 poverty-stricken Districts to produce specific products to maintain their charmed lives. As a way of re-affirming their dominance over the Districts (as well as provide a sadistic entertainment to the twisted rulers), they demand 2 &#34;tributes&#34; in the form of one boy and one girl between the ages of 12-18 to travel to the Capitol and be placed in a vast arena to survive and fight to the death. These televised trials are called &#34;The Hunger Games&#34;. This is the story of a 16 year old girl called Katniss Everdeen from District 12 who is one of the chosen.<br /><br />I heard about this book a few years ago in Stephen King's Entertainment Weekly column but I decided not to read it as it was labelled &#34;Young Adult&#34; (YA) (I'm not a teenager) and because it seemed like a knockoff &#34;Battle Royale&#34;, a superb Japanese film I'd seen in 2000 which has a very similar premise. Having read the book, in hindsight both of these reasons were ridiculous. The YA label is arbitrary at best and, reading it, I couldn't understand why this book is considered to be a teen book and not one for adults. Maybe the lack of swearing, sex, and overly descriptive violent scenes?<br /><br />And the &#34;Battle Royale&#34; argument (which seems to bother a number of reviewers), well was that the first book to feature kids on an island killing each other? Has no-one read &#34;Lord of the Flies&#34;? Shirley Jackson wrote maybe the best haunted house novel with &#34;The Haunting of Hill House&#34; - does that mean Richard Matheson's &#34;Hell House&#34; or Stephen King's &#34;The Shining&#34; are invalid because they followed in her wake? Or Yevgeny Zamyatin's &#34;We&#34; preceded both Aldous Huxley's &#34;Brave New World&#34; and George Orwell's &#34;Nineteen Eighty-Four&#34;, so do we discount Huxley and Orwell entirely? Just because a concept has been done before, doesn't mean it can't be done again - and done better at that.<br /><br />&#34;The Hunger Games&#34; is an exceptionally thrilling read. Suzanne Collins has crafted a compelling portrait of a world unrecognisable to our own, both futuristic and historic at varying times, but the best part of the book is, as it should be, the Games itself. Once Katniss enters the arena with the 23 other teens, I couldn't stop reading and finished the book on my second sitting, it was that exhilarating to read. Her battle to survive both on the basic level of eating and drinking what she could find, while escaping her would-be murderers, was a unique reading experience that I've never come across before and Collins does a fantastic writing job throughout. The survival part of the book echoed another excellent series of YA books I read when I was a teenager, Gary Paulsen's &#34;Hatchet&#34; books (highly recommended by the way), while the many Roman references made it seem like an extended teen &#34;Gladiator&#34;.<br /><br />I won't give away anything about the story here but suffice it to say that there are betrayals and killings that you don't see coming, and constant suspense throughout (even though you know Katniss makes it). Like I say, I don't know why this isn't considered an adult book but any adults reading this review should give this novel a go, ignore the YA label, it's very well written, very well-conceived, and an amazing action-thriller with elements of sci-fi thrown in. Excellent fun, I bought the other two books immediately after finishing this and can't wait to find out how this series continues.t mean it can't be done again - and done better at that.<br /><br />&#34;The Hunger Games&#34; is an exceptionally thrilling read. Feeling like chilling out, a bit blue, or winding down for the day, these tunes help sync with that mood.<br /><br />Other tunes are more playful and energetic, where Lana uses a fuller range of voice, as in \\"Off to the Races\\" and \\"Diet Mountain Dew,\\" both of which bounce along, but moderately--she's not trying to hit the disco dance floor here!<br /><br />One thing I like is use of very descriptive language, really painting pictures of things, as in \\"Off to the Races\\":  Swimming pool glimmering darling/ White bikini off with my red nail polish/ Watch me in the swimming pool / Bright blue ripples.<br /><br />\\"National Anthem\\" has a clever touch with the lead in of the sound of muted fireworks in the background.<br /><br />The rest of the songs that round out the album are okay, but don't stand out like the previous ones mentioned. 0385344228|4.0|Defending Jacob is ostensibly a legal thriller but it's as much a nuanced family drama as it is courtroom murder mystery.<br /><br />Jacob is a fourteen-year-old accused of murdering one of his classmates in the park on his way to school. Violence of this nature is completely foreign to this affluent Massachusetts community and Jacob's parents are not prepared for their neighbors disdain and the public microscope they're subjected to.  To make the situation even more scandalous, Jacob's father is the #2 man in the DA's office and was initially overseeing the investigation.<br /><br />The murder strains Jacob's parent's marriage and their relationship with their son. How well does any parent really know their child? Should they have known that their son was capable of this kind of violence?  Do they believe their son is innocent or guilty and how does that affect their role as a parent?  If they doubt their child's innocence are they being disloyal?  If their son is a killer, is it their fault?  Where should their allegiance be - with their son no matter what he's done or with a community that needs to be kept safe?  Is it fair for friends and neighbors to blame them for raising a monster?  To add fuel to this fire, family secrets are revealed that further tear the family apart with shame, guilt and blame.<br /><br />Jacob's DA father Andy provides the first-person narrative, the reliability of which is never completely clear.  There is a sense as you read that novel that Andy's narration may be selective, and that as a reader you may not be receiving a complete picture.  Woven through the story, in typed court reporter transcript, is a second narrative where Andy is on the stand as a grand jury witness, a year after the murder.  How this grand jury investigation fits into the story is revealed gradually and proves to be a very effective story-telling device.<br /><br />Jacob is something of an enigma. Most of our impressions of the boy come from conversations about him, not from scenes involving him.  We see him through the eyes of his father, whose inexorable faith in his son may or may not give us a reliable picture. The reader, and perhaps his parents, never have a clear picture of the boy.  Is he a typical teenager or is there something more to his sullen, withdrawn, self-absorbed persona?<br /><br />I had a few minor issues with the novel, behavior that I wasn't convinced rang completely true, but I found Defending Jacob to be a gripping page turner from start to finish.  It was the ending though that sealed the deal for me.  Defending Jacob finishes strong, one of the best endings to a suspense novel in recent memory.<br /><br />Landay does a solid job of developing the character of Andy, a proud man, tormented and struggling to hold his family together and protect them at all costs.  The novel may not appeal to action junkies, but it's a well-paced novel, with a compelling story that is certain to have parents of any teenager questioning how well they know their own child.  Landay is surprisingly good at capturing the rhythms of how teenagers talk (and post on-line), something that few adult authors do credibly.<br /><br />Defending Jacob is much more than just another legal thriller.  It's a powerful family drama and a social commentary that is certain to have readers pondering the issues the novel raises and asking themselves questions that aren't easy to answer.  4 Â½ stars.him, not from scenes involving him. 0743454537|4.0|\\"My Sister's Keeper,\\" (2005), by New York Times bestselling author Jodi Picoult, her eleventh novel, was a monster hit upon its publication.  It followed upon the heels of [[ASIN:1416583866 Second Glance: A Novel]], which is sorta kinda where I come in, as my mystery book club read, and I here reviewed  SECOND GLANCE.  However, whatever that book was, it wasn't much of a mystery, and I must admit I come to MY SISTER'S KEEPER only now, years after the rest of the world.  Which is a pity, as it's one powerful book; although they do say better late than never.<br /><br />SISTER'S KEEPER tells the story of the New England-based Fitzgeralds.  Brian, Dad, is a Providence, Rhode Island fire chief.  Mom Sara is a formerly practicing lawyer, now a full-time mother.  Kate, their eldest daughter, has a particularly virulent case of leukemia, cancer of the blood. Jesse, their son, who is always ignored due to Kate's never-ending medical drama, is getting himself into a lot of trouble.  And Anna, their youngest daughter, is a designer test-tube baby, created for the express purpose of supplying spare parts for her constantly medically yo-yoing sister.  The demand for parts has escalated, from stem cells at her birth, to bone marrow transplants( painful!), to, now, at thirteen, the `need' for her to donate one of her kidneys to her sister.  In fact, Anna has been in and out of the hospital almost as much as Kate and she has had enough.  She decides to sue her parents for medical emancipation, and sends shock waves round the world in so doing.  She hires a lawyer, Campbell Alexander to represent her; her Mom Sara represents the family's interests, and Judge De Salvo appoints Julia Romano as guardian ad litem to Anna, charging her with investigating the case and the family, and recommending what she thinks best for the child plaintiff's interests.<br /><br />No doubt this book can be called chick lit, as it is located within the female world of family.  But the book, as, SECOND GLANCE, and, I expect many of Picoult's works, shows her extraordinary ability to humanize and personalize the great relevant ethics issues of the day.  If you have a pulse, I don't see how you can help but be riveted by the medico/ethical/legal problems of the Fitzgerald family; and I will speak only for myself when I say I have no idea of the \\"correct\\" answer to the complex questions the book raises.<br /><br />The book was made into a substantial film of the same name in 2009,[[ASIN:B002ORTD46 My Sister's Keeper (2009)]].  It starred Cameron Diaz as Sara Fitzgerald, Abigail Breslin as Anna, and Alec Baldwin as the lawyer Campbell Alexander.  The movie was written for the screen and directed by Nick Cassavetes.<br /><br />Picoult was born in Nesconset, New York, a village so far out on Long Island, ordinarily considered a suburb of New York City's, that many people reckon it to be more New England-like than New York-like.  The author's family moved to New England when she was thirteen, and she seems to have largely stayed around there ever since: she took a master's degree at Harvard University, in Massachusetts.  This book, like SECOND GLANCE before it, is set in New England: I expect many of her books are.  She was the recipient of the 2003 New England Book Award for her entire body of work.<br /><br />The author, when queried about this novel, said, \\"I came about the idea for this novel through the back door of a previous one, Second Glance. While researching eugenics for that book, I learned that the American Eugenics Society -- the one whose funding dried up in the 1930s when the Nazis began to explore racial hygiene too -- used to be housed in Cold Spring Harbor, NY. Guess who occupies the same space, today? The Human Genome Project... which many consider `today's eugenics.' This was just too much of a coincidence for me, and I started to consider the way this massive, cutting edge science we're on the brink of exploding into was similar... and different from... the eugenics programs and sterilization laws in America in the 1930s. Once again, you've got science that is only as ethical as the people who are researching and implementing it -- and once again, in the wake of such intense scientific advancement, what's falling by the wayside are the emotions involved in the case by case scenarios. I heard about a couple in America that successfully conceived a sibling that was a bone marrow match for his older sister, a girl suffering from a rare form of leukemia. His cord blood cells were given to the sister, who is still (several years later) in remission. But I started to wonder... what if she ever, sadly, goes out of remission? Will the boy feel responsible? Will he wonder if the only reason he was born was because his sister was sick?\\"<br /><br />The reader may be interested to know that between 1929 and 1974, an estimated 7,600 people were sterilized by choice, force, or coercion under the North Carolina Eugenics Board. The program was not abolished until 1977; the legislature and governor of the state are even now discussing how much to award the victims of the program who still survive. It's still very much an issue in this state; the subject matter of SECOND GLANCE may well be even more relevant than Picoult realized. I read the original version of The Stand way back when right after it was published and was simply blown away.  I was a precocious young reader and no adult knew enough about this author to protect my innocent young mind.  Thank goodness!  Since then, I could never wait for the paperback version of his latest work. As I got older, I tired a little of the gore, but there was always the first half of the book which had the classic King suspense build-up that no one in the industry can seem to duplicate.<br /><br />Then came this book.  It's an atypical King novel, but a mind-bogglingly hold-on-to-your-hat page turner.  WARNING:  Do not buy this book if you have any important event coming up in your life. B001V7UXG2|5.0|Whenever I'm down in the dumps, one of the things I can usually rely on to put me in a better mood is \\"Jeeves and Wooster\\".  I don't know what it is, but there's something about this show that gives me a warm, cheerful, naÃ¯vely optimistic outlook on life -- the sort of outlook that Bertie Wooster seems to have, at least in those rare moments when, with a little help from Jeeves, he manages to escape being bullied, blackmailed, or betrothed.<br /><br />In case you're not already familiar with \\"Jeeves and Wooster\\", it is a British TV show that originally ran from 1990-93 on ITV, and also aired in the U.S. as part of the PBS series \\"Masterpiece Theatre\\".  The show is based on the Jeeves stories of author P.G. Wodehouse; and it stars the fabulous comedy duo of Fry and Laurie in the title roles.<br /><br />Set in England (and a few episodes in New York) in the 1920s, the show recounts the misadventures of Bertram Wilberforce \\"Bertie\\" Wooster (Hugh Laurie) and his devoted manservant Reginald Jeeves (Stephen Fry).  Bertie and his friends and relatives seem to have a knack for getting themselves into trouble; and they usually have to rely on Jeeves to get them out of it.  Bertie Wooster is a hapless, yet loveable, upper-class twit, who just wants to enjoy a carefree life.  Unfortunately, his kind heart, dim wit, and total lack of backbone make it impossible for him to avoid getting caught up in other people's drama, which he usually ends up making worse.  Fortunately for him, he has Jeeves.  With his stoical unflappability, his erudite wisdom, and his encyclopedic knowledge of even the most obscure bits of factual information, Jeeves is the man you want by your side in any crisis.  And, since Wooster's life seems to be one crisis after another, he is very lucky to have Jeeves as his \\"gentleman's personal gentleman\\".  The always-proper, always-dignified, always-faithful Jeeves can be counted on to do what's best for his master, even in the most absurd of situations.<br /><br/>Sir Pelham Grenville Wodehouse (1881-1975) wrote nearly 100 books -- including full-length novels and collections of short stories -- many of which recounted humorous tales about members of the British aristocracy and gentry during the early 20th century, with many of his characters appearing in multiple stories.  His most famous stories were the ones about a clever valet named Jeeves who was constantly having to find ways to get his master, a wealthy loafer named Bertie Wooster, out of trouble.  But characters from the Jeeves stories also appeared in many of Wodehouse's other books.  He basically created an entire fictional universe of wacky characters, all of whom seemed to be connected to each other in some way, allowing him to write stories that could feature just about any combination of those characters.  But his most popular characters, by far, were Jeeves and Wooster.  This series is a pretty faithful adaptation of several of Wodehouse's Jeeves stories for television.  The stories were dramatized for television by the late Clive Exton (1930-2007), the same brilliant screenwriter who was responsible for dramatizing 31 of Agatha Christie's stories for the wonderful ITV television series \\"Poirot\\".<br /><br />The humor in \\"Jeeves and Wooster\\" is a cross between a comedy of manners and a farce.  It caricatures the members of the British upper classes (especially the younger members) as eccentric, not very bright, out of touch with the real world, self-absorbed, spoiled, lazy, petulant, scheming, amoral, uncultured, bumbling, foolish, and almost totally dependant on their servants -- who are invariably portrayed as smarter, wiser, and classier than their masters -- to insure that they maintain the proper standards of decorum for people of their social standing.  But most of the humor comes from the wacky schemes that these upper-class halfwits come up with in order to accomplish their selfish goals, which invariably get them into trouble.  And, try as he mightto avoid it, Bertie Wooster always seems to get caught right in the middle of all this trouble, and has to rely on Jeeves to get him out.  Fortunately, Jeeves is so clever that he can almost always figure out a way to resolve the problem; though the resolution often comes at the expense of Bertie's pride and dignity.<br /><br />Anyway, this is a great show; and I highly recommend it.  Fry and Laurie are brilliant in it, as they are in everything they are in, especially when they work together.  There are 23 episodes in all, over four seasons (5 episodes in the first season, 6 episodes in each of the remaining seasons).  The only complaint I have is that most of the secondary characters who appear in more than one season are not played by the same actor throughout all four seasons.  You get used to seeing one actor in a particular role, and then in the next season a different actor might be playing that role.  It can be quite jarring.  But that's my only complaint.  It's a wonderful show, with lots of clever humor and witty dialogue, fun characters, and even some really good music (if you like the music of the Jazz Age as much as I do).  Get it and watch it.  You won't regret it.<br /><br />I don't want to give away any spoilers -- it's better for new viewers to discover all the fun things about this show first hand by actually watching for themselves rather than reading about them in a review -- but I do want to pass along one little tidbit of knowledge that might enhance your appreciation of certain aspects of the show.  It has to do with money.  Because many of the characters in this show are quite wealthy, money is mentioned a lot throughout the series.  But, since these stories are set in Britain in the 1920s, American viewers today might not have a clue what sort of sums are being discussed.  For example, when a judge fines Bertie Â£5, or when someone asks to borrow Â£10 from Bertie to pay for drinks at a bar, these sound to us like fairly trivial amounts.  But,actually, that was quite a lot of money back then.  Five pounds sterling in 1925 would be roughly equivalent to $300 in today's money; and Â£10 then would be worth about $600 now.  So, whenever you hear characters talking about sums of money in British pounds (\\"quid\\"), multiply that amount by 60 and you'll get a rough approximation of how much they're talking about in today's American dollars.  For amounts less than Â£1, which are measured in shillings (\\"bob\\") and pence (where 1 shilling = 12 pence, and 1 pound = 20 shillings = 240 pence) just keep in mind that one shilling in 1925 would be roughly equivalent to $3 today, and one British penny back then would have about the same value as an American quarter does today.  When they are talking about American currency (e.g. in the episodes set in New York), just multiply everything by 13 to get a very rough estimate of what it would be worth in today's dollars (e.g. $1 then = about $13 now).  Understanding this will help you better appreciate the obscene amounts of money that are routinely tossed around by the wealthy characters in these stories, such as when Bertie bets Â£100 on a race.<br /><br />So, now that you have this little bit of knowledge to aid in your enjoyment, you're ready to begin your fun adventure with \\"Jeeves and Wooster\\".  Trust me, you'll love every minute of it.  And, if you're in a bad mood, it just might cheer you up.

A virus is borne not because God wants it, not because there is someone who devised it and sent it other people in the mail, but because a few simple accidents in nature occur.  What ensues is a gruelling and very human battle against this disease, as Soderberg follows the head of the CDC (Lawrence Fishburne), a simple man (Matt Damon) who loses his wife in the opening scene (you get to see Gwyneth Paltrow get sick and die, and unlike in [[ASIN:B000050FEN Seven (New Line Platinum Series)]] you get to see the details).<br /><br />The beauty and terror of this masterfully crafted film is the lack of sentimentality.  There are heroes and villains but only *human* heroes and villains. Jude Law plays the truly chilling Alan Krumwiede, a wannabe writer/popular blogger who takes advantage of the situation for money and prestige, almost a Hitleresque figure. It's a bit like watching your relatives get it too, and watching how *they* would react.  Recommended.

Martin is producing literary crack with his stories of the battles and the intrigues of this fictional land. I almost referred to Westeros as a \\"mythical\\" land, but that would have fallen short as a description. Mythic has always seemed to refer to larger-than-life characters. In A Clash of Kings the characters are life-sized and very human. They are also very complicated, neither entirely good nor entirely evil. Even when they may do evil things, their motivations seem truly human and understandable. There are no \\"classic\\" bad guys who chortle with glee when their nefarious plans come to fruition. I even found myself empathizing with Cersei and her son, King Joffery, who are arguably the least likable characters.<br /><br />Not only does Martin produce fascinating characters, but the world of Westeros is masterfully built. Rather than throw tons of exposition at readers all at once, Martin lets the kingdoms blossom slowly and we are constantly making new discoveries. Take, for example, his decriptions of the religions of Westeros. In A Game of Thrones he offers some details of the new gods and the old gods. In A Clash of Kings he adds new details that enhance what has come before. The technique adds layers of depth to the story.<br /><br />Likewise, the world Martin created is a morally neutral one. Evil sometimes triumphs. Good sometimes get corrupted. Even the most noble characters can die (as we learned in the first book). Martin isn't afraid to turn the conventional wisdom of fantasy on its head (or even shake them up, toss them in a literary food processor, and serve up the results to readers). As a result - at least in the first two works - you get a series weaving traditional fantasy threads through a world that feels very natural and organic.<br /><br />I've purposely avoided discussing the plot of A Clash of Kings. That's partly because any synopsis I could write would pale in comparison to Martin's complicated, yet readable, plotting. That he can keeps us reading while his plot runs from battlefields to bedchambers to the froze land beyond The Wall to the Red Waste across the Narrow Sea is nothing short of amazing. Couple that with some of the most fascinating characters I've ever encountered between the covers of a book and you have an absolute winner. Tyrion, Jon Stark, Arya, Caitlyn ... I'd almost swear these were flesh-and-blood people who allowed me to look over their shoulders.<br /><br />It's nearly impossible to heap enough praise on Martin's series, but allow me one more observation: rather than taking a break after completing A Clash of Kings, I've already downloaded the next in the series, A Storm of Swords, and will eagerly dive into it tonight.r even shake them up, toss them in a literary food processor, and serve up the results to readers). 0765350386|3.0|Mistborn is a decent story, though definitely not the best that I have read.<br /><br />One of the big selling points for this novel is its magic system.  To summarize, there are two primary \\"forms of magic\\":  Allomancy and Feruchemy.  Allomancy allows a person to consume metallic alloys that grant abilities.  Steel, for example, allows a person to \\"push\\" other metals away.  Zinc, on the other hand, allows its consumer to enhance certain emotions in another person.  Overall, there are 8 basic metals, each with its own function.  Most individuals can only 'burn' one type of metal, but there are occasionally those who can burn all of the metals (these people are called Mistborn).  Feruchemy, is a bit different.  Instead of drawing energy from metals as an Allomancer does, a Feruchemist can store certain attributes (such as sight, strength, knowledge, etc.) into a metal and draw upon them later.  I was thoroughly impressed with how well Sanderson explores each of these forms of magic.  I also appreciated how original they were.<br /><br />Unfortunately, I have to say that setting isn't all that interesting.  Maybe I am the only one who feels this way, but I can't help but to feel an overwhelming sense of..smallness to the world.  I know that this was probably intentional on Sanderson's part as he was trying to create a world that barely escaped an all out apocalypse, but even still, I personally prefer stories with larger scale worlds.  This particular point may not be an issue with some readers, but it made me a little claustrophobic.<br /><br />The character development wasn't the greatest either.  The main character, Vin, is pretty well done.  But other than that, most of the characters are fairly..boring and predictable.  Anytime something reckless happens, you immediately know that it was Kelsier.  Anytime someone comes up with a plan, you can expect a generic, sarcastic comment from Breeze.  I dunno..I was just a bit bored with all of the characters because whatyou see is what you get.  This may also contribute to the sense of smallness that I described earlier.  I'm currently on book 3, and so far, Sanderson hasn't done much to change this..<br /><br />I also hate that there aren't any unexpected twists and turns.  I understand that many authors like to direct their stories so that the intended outcome inevitably happens..but that is so boring!  Before reading any of Sanderson's work, I read the first two novels in A Song of Ice and Fire.  Talk about a series with a bunch of twists and turns!  You never know what to expect in those and you never feel 'safe'.  Sanderson, however, doesn't add very many..unexpected twists.  He basically spells out everything that is going to happen in the next few chapters through dialogue...and if there is the possibility of something unexpected happening, he uses his lovely little characters to explain why everything will go as planned (and, in the end, it always does).  And if there are complications, they are usually fairly easy to predict.<br /><br />It has taken me a very long time to get through this series (I started it in January D: ).  I really enjoy books that require a lot of thinking, and unfortunately, I don't feel like Sanderson delivers much in this area.  While his magic system is unique, his world building could use some work, his characters are a tad cliche, and the storyline is predictable.<br /><br />Sorry to offend any fans :P

B004SC8XHI|5.0|There are three things that must be said about this recording:<br /><br />First, this is not a gimmick!  This is not \\"Living Strings Play Beatles.\\"  It's more akin to hearing \\"Round Midnight\\" by Miles Davis, rather than Thelonious Monk, or perhaps hearing \\"Appalachian Spring\\" conducted by Michael Tilson Thomas, rather than Aaron Copland.  It's just as valid as the original, paying the original due respect but with a slightly different artistic twist.<br /><br />Second, this recording shows the true virtuosity of the four guys who made up the group.  The musical vision is that of Booker T. Jones; in the liner notes he states how he was knocked out by the musicality of Abbey Road, and wanted to record the music with the group.  His keyboards are creative and concise, bringing out a visceral response to this music.  But the other three players are allowed the space to do their thing, too, and they are incredible.  This recording shows why all four of them, Booker T., Steve Cropper, Duck Dunn, and certainly not least Al Jackson, Jr., are now considered icons of soul music.  The funny thing is, this music is closer to jazz than soul/funk; call it jazz with a soul heart.<br /><br />Third, the 24-bit remastering by Joe Tarantino is amazing.  This is the best I have ever heard BTMG sound.  Although the recording was made in 1970, the sound is up to more modern standards (although the bonus tracks, covers of Beatles songs issued on earlier BTMG albums, are a noticeably \\"older\\" sound).  I hope Concord and Tarantino remaster more of the old Stax output.<br /><br />This album has immediately jumped up onto my top-10 albums to take to a desert island.  It's that good.

014312093X|5.0|The problem with Le Carre for some readers is two-fold; for one thing, he is quintessentially British, and his writing uses many British idioms and words that are nearly meaningless to mnay American readers, unfamiliar with the intimate details of UK society and social history. For example, if you don't know what the New Year's Lists (aka Honours Lists) are, you won't follow why that list resulted in some of the people working for the Circus (nickname for the intelligence agency) being awarded medals. Likewise, if you don't know about the British system of public schools and how the social classes traverse the educational system, you will not realize why that has a bearing on who runs the intelligence department.<br /><br />And the second issue is that Le Carre's style is more literary--the action is often portrayed by a character relating a story to others, not actually happening in real time. You really have to immerse yourself as if you were sitting next to the characters, listening to them talk and trying not only to follow the conversation, but to get inside their heads, their attitudes, pain, conflict, and their forming suspicions. He stops to give us a glimpse of the weather (which always has an emotional meaning) and also takes us into the internal dialog going on in the head of one or two of the protagonists.<br /><br />This is what makes John Le Carre's writing so rewarding for me--I get sucked into the very mind of George Smiley. And this novel, the first in the Smiley series, is a terrific tale of betrayal and double-crossing that hits in the worst possible place; a traitor at the very highest levels of a nation's intelligence agency. Smiley, who has been sacked in a reorganization of the agency, tries to become a retired \\"has been\\" but is drafted by colleagues who suspect the \\"Circus\\" has been co-opted by a Soviet mole and need his vast memory, his logic and his knowledge of agency activities at the end of WWII and the Cold War to flush out the traitor. And it's a dangerous game; people have already been killed, and not \\"cleanly\\" nor quickly, either.<br /><br />The story eases you in by introducing Jim through the eyes of a \\"new boy\\", a hapless young kid with few skills who has been deemed possibly deficient in intellect, but who has a tremendous ability to observe tiny details (is this a mirror of a young George Smiley?) We watch Jim through Bill's eyes and simply get a description of someone who has a mysterious past, a bad back injury and who has some unique skills despite his downtrodden air. From this idyllic beginning, we then move on to Smiley's world and are taken down into the labyrinth of the Cold War trenches.  As the story unfolds, we follow Smiley as he is able to finally investigate his earlier suspicions about traitors at highest levels, the very suspicions that probably got him sacked in the first place.<br /><br />I couldn't put it down. If you read it, just don't expect fast moving action or punchy writing. This is slow, deliberate, sometimes elliptical and vague, but always fascinating. For me, Le Carre remains the master of this genre and perhaps, one who is unique in his style.And it's a dangerous game; people have already been killed, and not \\"cleanly\\" nor quickly, either.<br /><br />The story eases you in by introducing Jim through the eyes of a \\"new boy\\", a hapless young kid with few skills who has been deemed possibly deficient in intellect, but who has a tremendous ability to observe tiny details (is this a mirror of a young George Smiley?) We watch Jim through Bill's eyes and simply get a description of someone who has a mysterious past, a bad back injury and who has some unique skills despite his downtrodden air. From this idyllic beginning, we then move on to Smiley's world and are taken down into the labyrinth of the Cold War trenches.  As the story unfolds, we follow Smiley as he is able to finally investigate his earlier suspicions about traitors at highest levels, the very suspicions that probably got him sacked in the first place.<br /><br />I couldn't put it down. If you read it, just don't expect fast moving action or punchy writing. This is slow, deliberate, sometimes elliptical and vague, but always fascinating. For me, Le Carre remains the master of this genre and perhaps, one who is unique in his style.

B0073XORCY|4.0|I'm enjoying the hell out of TAAB2. It's definitely not the original, but in an odd way, it's on par with some of Tull's 60's and early 70's work. That's not to say TAAB2 is the return of Tull's glory days (it's no Aqualung or Minstrel in the Gallery), but it beats the hell out of Rock Island, Catfish Rising, and such.<br /><br />TAAB2 sounds far more like Jethro Tull than an Ian Anderson solo work. I love his mellow, friendly albums like Rupi's Dance, but TAAB2 is far closer to, say, Warchild and/or Too Old to Rock 'n' Roll than it is Rupi or Secret Language of Birds.<br /><br />Diehard Tull fans might miss the contribution of Martin Barre on TAAB2, but it works without him. The album, while not quite as flowing as the original TAAB (that is to say, it feels more like a concept album of separate songs woven together, rather than one huge, long track), contains plenty of callbacks to its forerunner coupled with music that sounds inspired by the original.<br /><br />Reeking of effort, which the later Jethro Tull albums lacked (at least, from the end of the 1970's until J-Tull Dot Com), TAAB2 isn't a must-have, but it's enjoyable and well worth the cost.<br /><br />Final note: If you're unsure whether you'll like it, or lack a good Dolby Digital 5.1 system, just download it or get the single CD. The special edition's 5.1 mix doesn't add a whole lot to the experience; I've heard better 5.1 mixes.

Close Encounters was a goofy con job with trashy leitmotives, gauze and blurry lighting. ET was more of the same with lots of cute kids. Star Wars, Indiana Jones, Jurassic Park, in spite of theatric high marks for execution, were mindless homages to serialized kiddie junk from the 30s to the 50s.<br /><br />Schindlers List turned that all around. It had substance. It put some of that cinemagraphic education to work in a frightenly grim look into the face of real horror. There have been other acheivments ( and flops ) since then.<br /><br />I don't know the childs book basis. Usually literary transpositions wind up a drawback anyway. I do know literature, films, Ralph Vaughn-Williams and Delius. This movie pulls from period wells very effectively to create ambience.<br /><br />There is a lot going on in the structure of the movie. At the heart of all the stories there is that mystic love of the horse. Few movies have captured that as well as Black Stallion. Warhorse has done that for me. The animals in this movie are amazingly engaged in the tale.<br /><br />The pace is gentile, nostalgic and manages to try to see hope and love in tragic circumstances of a horrible war.  Perhaps that is melodramatic. Perhaps it is too optimistic to see things come out right in the end; with so many characters chosing to make honorable and loving decisions.<br /><br />The story here avoids the overwhelming brutality of Schindler's or Private Ryan. There are the French Grandfather and granddaughter struggling to survive too close to the war, who recover the stallions only to see the arrival of revenous foraging enemies taking all away from them.<br /><br />The tale comes to a climax when survivors meet in the last half hour of the film. Keep a hankie (s) at the ready as you will repeatedly need them.<br /><br />It's a beautifully crafted film. Hat's off to everyone involved in the project. As with too many Speilberg projects you wind up having to suspend some judgement. How this horse manages to survive nearly four years of war when everyting around him dies is utter nonsense. It's still Indiana Jones and Star Wars schtick.<br /><br />But there is a lifetime of craft conjoined to this formula. My skepticism has been conquered.<br /><br />Five stars from me. I loved it.
